  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/762830927" />
    <a title="Jacob McLaws" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15283898-jacob-mclaws"><img alt="Jacob McLaws" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587025105p2/15283898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/762830927?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob McLaws" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob McLaws" href="/user/show/15283898-jacob-mclaws">Jacob McLaws</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780465050659" href="/book/show/17290807-the-design-of-everyday-things">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15283898-jacob-mclaws?shelf=design">design</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer762830927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17315064091248924621">This a required read for anyone who wants to design things for humans to use, but it was more like a textbook than I hoped when I picked it up. Lots of design vocabulary and lots of fairly common-sense principles. Don Norman is definitely one of the early design thinkers and this is where he talks about it all. <br/><br/>Big takeaways: <br/>Signifiers and feedback are key in designing something. The user needs to be able to quickly understand what it can do (affordances) and get immediate and appropriate feedb</span>
  <span id="freeText17315064091248924621" style="display:none">This a required read for anyone who wants to design things for humans to use, but it was more like a textbook than I hoped when I picked it up. Lots of design vocabulary and lots of fairly common-sense principles. Don Norman is definitely one of the early design thinkers and this is where he talks about it all. <br /><br />Big takeaways: <br />Signifiers and feedback are key in designing something. The user needs to be able to quickly understand what it can do (affordances) and get immediate and appropriate feedback when they do something.<br /><br />Human Centered Design Process: all about observing to understand the problem, ideating solutions, rapid prototyping and testing. You should test with small groups at first so that you have lots of opportunity to refine and iterate. I especially liked the part about using the Wizard of Oz technique: manually handling the backend yourself and letting the user think you are automating it. I think that's often the best way to test whether something will gain traction. Doesn't work for everything, but sometimes it's very useful. <br /><br />When there's something wrong, humans aren't using things wrong, designers are designing poorly. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17315064091248924621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_387919178" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/387919178" />
    <a title="Tracey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/841949-tracey"><img alt="Tracey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325394892p2/841949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/387919178?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracey" href="/user/show/841949-tracey">Tracey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781452654126" href="/book/show/12071117-the-design-of-everyday-things">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=craft">craft</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer387919178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12637326874353103735">The main question in my mind after listening to this audiobook is easily enough answered: <i>How old IS this book, anyhow?</i> In the introduction the author talks about how the book isn't dated. Well, it was originally published in 1988. One of the pieces of technology most discussed is the videocassette recorder. The VCR. The computers being discussed are about a step beyond the ones that were capable of adding three numbers together using a bank of systems that would fill a room. <br/><br/>Some of the book is</span>
  <span id="freeText12637326874353103735" style="display:none">The main question in my mind after listening to this audiobook is easily enough answered: <i>How old IS this book, anyhow?</i> In the introduction the author talks about how the book isn't dated. Well, it was originally published in 1988. One of the pieces of technology most discussed is the videocassette recorder. The VCR. The computers being discussed are about a step beyond the ones that were capable of adding three numbers together using a bank of systems that would fill a room. <br /><br />Some of the book is relevant no matter what, as the prologue or forward or introduction or whatever it was points out. But not all of it is. <br /><br />Much of the point of this book is: "When people have trouble with something, it's not their fault. It's the fault of the design." And I don't buy it. Maybe it's because I have less faith in humanity than the author does, but – well, I've seen it (including, to be honest, in myself). I did not like the book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43889.Wizard_s_First_Rule__Sword_of_Truth___1_" title="Wizard's First Rule (Sword of Truth, #1) by Terry Goodkind" rel="nofollow noopener">Wizard's First Rule</a>, but something I love and use all the time is its explication of Wizard's First Rule: "People Are Idiots". Yes, it should be obvious whether a door needs to be pushed or pulled to get the thing open – but in most if not all of the cases I've seen it's not actively hidden. In my experience, people just don't pay attention. People don't read. <br /><br />Example: I can't tell you how many emails I've sent, only to have to reiterate some or all of it almost immediately. I used to run an international online-based Secret Santa, and every year after the emails went out I braced myself for the slew of responses asking questions that were answered in the initial email. Because people don't read. <br /><br />I've learned that when I ask two questions or provide two pieces of information in an email, the second one is going to go completely unnoticed. More than two? Forget it. Now, I've long ago learned that my tendency to wordiness won't fly in business emails – I've learned to pare it down. Still, people don't read. <br /><br />Recent example: in reply to a question from one of my bosses, I wrote "I’ve attached [three pieces of documentation for a delivery]; it looks like there was no delivery ticket created." That was the first line of my email. One of them replied with "Do we have delivery ticket?" I sat and stared at it for a couple of minutes, thinking of and discarding possible responses, and then just wrote back "There was no delivery ticket, as far as I can see". I just don't understand.<br /><br />Example: I can't tell you how many people go up to the fax machine in the office and ask whether paperwork has to be face-up or face-down. (The owner of the company asks every time.) (Every. Time.) How do you work in offices as long as these people have without learning that there is a little graphic on the machine to answer just that important question. (I also can't tell you how many blank faxes I've received over the years, because people a) didn't read and b) didn't ask, and just faxed away. Upside down.) The design is just fine: the question is answered. I'm not sure how else it could be addressed; bright colors or flashing lights? Or big letters? Nah. It's fine. People are idiots. <br /><br />So your car radio is difficult to use while driving? Here's a thought: <i>Don't use it while driving.</i> You might want to watch the road instead. <br /><br />The author talks about an expensive hoity toity Italian washing machine that was so badly designed the owners were afraid to touch it. "Why did they buy it?" the author asks. Well, because it's an expensive hoity toity Italian machine – and they're stupid. They wanted conspicuous consumption, or got snowed by a salesman who perceived their weakness. Plus they probably hire someone to do their laundry anyway, or at any rate seem to be able to afford to. <br /><br />And the author complains about the problems inherent in lowering a projection screen in a lecture hall – but it sounds like the hall long predates slide projectors. The projector had to be installed in the place long after the fact, and in such a way (I would assume) so as not to do any mischief to the structure or artistry of the room. So – yeah, it's not perfect. It doesn't exist in perfect conditions. Work with it. Or hold your lectures somewhere else. <br /><br />And the author complains about senseless instructions for those VCR's, and all I could think was, well, they're often translated, badly, from Japanese. <br /><br />The author talks about a design feature – or not – in an Audi which allowed the sunroof to be closed without the ignition key in place, but only if an odd sequence of steps were taken. Why, he asks, was it such a peculiar combination of steps? Well, a) because it was accidental, and/or b) because a non-peculiar combination might result in an accidental opening of the sunroof when you really didn't want it open. (I say "you" because I'll never so much as sit in an Audi.) <br /><br />Now, I do agree with the basic premise of the book. Of course an object should be designed so that it's not difficult to use. But … well, see, over the sink in my apartment there are three switches. When I had a tour of the place I was told that the one on the left controlled the light, the one in the middle controlled the garbage disposal, and the last one was for the dishwasher. When I moved in a little while later it took about five minutes' trial and error to work it out again. Now I don't have to think about it. I don't need a huge sign on the wall. Figure it out yourself: you'll probably remember it longer. "Control/alt/delete" isn't an intuitive command for the computer – but the reason for that is pretty sensible: it's not something that can be done using one or two close-set keys … because it's not something you want to do accidentally. And once it's learned, it's easy enough to remember. <br /><br />Okay, go back to the whole door thing. The author admits that he has problems with doors. And I get it – if there's no label on a door it can be hard to know whether you're supposed to push or pull or whatever. But – at least nowadays – I think every door I see in a public venue has a little sign. Or - know what? If you're meant to pull, there's probably a handle, and if you're meant to push there's probably a bar. And … I'm sorry, I can't muster up a whole lot of sympathy for the person who pulls on a door that says "push", or vice versa – including me. Honestly, I have little patience with anyone (including me) who doesn't read the damn directions. <br /><br />I also don't have a lot of patience for someone, like the man in this book, who goes out and buys a massively expensive Italian washing machine without making sure he understands how to use it. Yes, that can be blamed on the design; it can also be blamed on the salesman seeing dollar signs, and on the fact that any instruction manual is probably translated from the Italian – and on a level of carelessness and lack of preparedness by the buyer. I'm sorry – if you don't put in a certain level of research into a big purchase, you deserve what you end up with. <br /><br />If I need, for example, to make a spreadsheet do something I don't know how to do, I don't write a letter to MicroSoft complaining about the poor design of Excel. I figure it out, or I look it up. If I don't know what a word means, I look it up. You know the adage that God helps those who help themselves? I like it. I work with people who don't bother to try to solve any problem for themselves. If they don't know how to do something, they sit in their seats and yell like children for help – literally. It sounds like the author is in favor of this attitude – everything should be obvious, and if it's not you're entitled to squawk. It's learned helplessness. <br /><br />My feeling on this is basically that if I can figure it out, or look it up, anyone can – and damn well should. <br /><br />And read my damned email, jackass. <br /><br />So, no – technology of any sort should not be intentionally or incidentally obscure. But also, and equally, people should be able to learn and follow the instructions that are present and hone their deductive instincts. It's an ability that will only ever make life easier.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12637326874353103735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_273360203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273360203" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/145381-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386647011p2/145381.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273360203?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/145381-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Brian by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/73-michael-economy">Michael Economy</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/145381-brian?shelf=principal-engineer-reading-list">principal-engineer-reading-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273360203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4107920963713045838">(4.0) Some good stuff in here, though it's certainly dated<br/><br/>I'll be looking up some of his other books to see if he's as good at predicting and suggesting product improvements as he was back then.<br/><br/>I think he makes concrete some really common sense ways to approach and analyze designs of products that humans use. It's certainly entertaining to point out ridiculous products, interfaces etc., but that's kind of 'negative design': what not to do. That doesn't actually help you do it right. Fortunately,</span>
  <span id="freeText4107920963713045838" style="display:none">(4.0) Some good stuff in here, though it's certainly dated<br /><br />I'll be looking up some of his other books to see if he's as good at predicting and suggesting product improvements as he was back then.<br /><br />I think he makes concrete some really common sense ways to approach and analyze designs of products that humans use. It's certainly entertaining to point out ridiculous products, interfaces etc., but that's kind of 'negative design': what not to do. That doesn't actually help you do it right. Fortunately, he does spend a fair amount of time on how to do it right. So some good stuff to summarize:<br /><br />* Make the controls/interactive elements visible: they won't used if they're not noticed<br />* Use cultural, intuitive clues to suggest the function of elements...e.g. choose materials, shapes, colors appropriately<br />* Try to make the interactive elements map to the functions they perform, particularly easy if there is some spatial component to what is controlled, arrange the elements in the same arrangement as their actions<br />* Think of the steps that will occur when a user interacts with a product:<br /> -- user forms a goal<br /> -- user translates the goal into smaller, more concrete 'intentions'<br /> -- user enacts the intention as best he can<br /> -- the system responds<br /> -- the user tries to interpret the response<br /> -- 'bugs' can occur anywhere in this chain, identifying the source can help identify the solution<br />* Make sure there's feedback when user does something. If the product's state has changed, make sure user can tell. Readable displays can be helpful here<br />* Users will make mistakes. Expect them and make them reversible, low impact<br />* If you need instructions for new user to operate, you're probably doing it wrong<br />* Try to use constraints to limit the wrong actions user can take<br /> -- e.g. 3.5" floppy disks can only be inserted one way into drive (though they look square and top not much different from bottom)<br /> -- cultural constraints can be used as well as physical constraints<br />* as last resort, turn to arbitrary standards (so even if something not intuitive, user only has to learn once and can apply to all similar devices)<br /> -- e.g. QWERTY keyboard<br />* how to use technology best to improve products/processes:<br /> -- simplify tasks, but leave them largely the same (don't automate away key steps that users will forget occur and can't troubleshoot when something's wrong)<br /> -- make things visible that weren't visible before so state is easier to track<br /> -- design for error, don't blame "human error" when unintuitive/broken interface leads to disaster (e.g. three mile island)<br /><br />So I think a few of these can be explicit steps to take when evaluating a design:<br />* are the relevant features visible? is feedback visible?<br />* is it clear what the mapping is from interface to resulting actions?<br />* when user interacts with each control, is there appropriate feedback?<br />* can user identify when he's made an error? can he undo the error? are the 'human errors' ever catastrophic?<br />* are there constraints you can apply to reduce possibility of error?<br /><br />He also made some cool predictions/product requests, which makes me want to read some of his more recent stuff:<br />* the windows/macintosh user interface would take off<br />* calendar/reminder book would be electronic and fit in your pocket (but didn't think they'd BE the phone...talked about connecting the calendar to the phone...but well on his way to asking for the smartphone)<br />* how big hypertext would get, mostly in the context of books/media...not sure he really thought of the Web as the logical extension though<br />* it also seems that his line of thinking was adopted into the types of user testing that i'm familiar with...let naive users play with the product with no guidance and see what sense they make of it. what mistakes do they make? when were they surprised by how the product behaved? why?<br /><br />Only negative bits were that there was some material in the middle about theory of mind, memory, psychology of errors etc. that I didn't think was all that relevant. Interesting, perhaps, but a little out of place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4107920963713045838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_594895290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/594895290" />
    <a title="Eduardo Rocha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6314428-eduardo-rocha"><img alt="Eduardo Rocha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546564629p2/6314428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/594895290?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eduardo Rocha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eduardo Rocha" href="/user/show/6314428-eduardo-rocha">Eduardo Rocha</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer594895290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16605169536938611731">This book is amazing. You'll never look at another door or faucet in the sameway.<br/>If you take anything from this book, it is these 7 principles of making a difficult design task an easy one.<br/><br/>1. Use both knowledge in the world and knowledge in the head.<br/>2. Simplify the structure of tasks.<br/>3. Make things visible: bridge the gulfs of Execution and Evaluation.<br/>4. Get the mappings right.<br/>5. Exploit the power of constraints, both natural and artificial.<br/>6. Design for error.<br/>7. When all else fails, standardize</span>
  <span id="freeText16605169536938611731" style="display:none">This book is amazing. You'll never look at another door or faucet in the sameway.<br />If you take anything from this book, it is these 7 principles of making a difficult design task an easy one.<br /><br />1. Use both knowledge in the world and knowledge in the head.<br />2. Simplify the structure of tasks.<br />3. Make things visible: bridge the gulfs of Execution and Evaluation.<br />4. Get the mappings right.<br />5. Exploit the power of constraints, both natural and artificial.<br />6. Design for error.<br />7. When all else fails, standardize.<br /><br />Altough, the reason for the 4 star reviews, is that the book is a bit outdated. Not the design principles, even after 20 year since the book was published, some things are poorly designed. But the book use examples like telephones and the future of publishing, like Hyperlinks(!).</span>

<div id="review_943818767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/943818767" />
    <a title="Lacey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6045589-lacey"><img alt="Lacey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589213243p2/6045589.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/943818767?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lacey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lacey" href="/user/show/6045589-lacey">Lacey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6045589-lacey?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6045589-lacey?shelf=design">design</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer943818767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8644468110921613903">I'm just going to be real. This book was incredibly boring. I picked it up because I was told it was a classic of the field and would be useful to have in my reading repertoire. And truthfully, the only reason I gave it two stars instead of one is because of the impact this book has clearly had on the design field. I'm sure at the time of its original release, this book was light years ahead of others in the way that it thought about design. I can definitely see how its concepts have become a ma</span>
  <span id="freeText8644468110921613903" style="display:none">I'm just going to be real. This book was incredibly boring. I picked it up because I was told it was a classic of the field and would be useful to have in my reading repertoire. And truthfully, the only reason I gave it two stars instead of one is because of the impact this book has clearly had on the design field. I'm sure at the time of its original release, this book was light years ahead of others in the way that it thought about design. I can definitely see how its concepts have become a main part of every design education. <br /><br />But ultimately, I thought this book was too wordy. It said in hundreds of pages what, in my opinion, could probably have been said in less. A lot of it is very common sense, in that most people don't even really think about the concepts. <br /><br />Altogether, probably still good that I read it. But I'd be lying if I said that I didn't skim a majority of it for the important bits. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8644468110921613903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19085988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19085988" />
    <a title="Jon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040333-jon"><img alt="Jon" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19085988?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jon" href="/user/show/1040333-jon">Jon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">designers, computer scientists, engineers</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19085988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12705477810733874356">The book introduces basic psychological concepts from areas such as cognitive psychology and ties them into usability and design.<br/><br/>Even though the book feels a bit outdated (they talk about rotary phones and old sewing machines), all the principles covered in the book still apply today.<br/><br/>Even though the book was written with things in mind that most of us won't necessarily use anymore (such as the problem of threading a projector), the principles are still useful to know when designing modern-day th</span>
  <span id="freeText12705477810733874356" style="display:none">The book introduces basic psychological concepts from areas such as cognitive psychology and ties them into usability and design.<br /><br />Even though the book feels a bit outdated (they talk about rotary phones and old sewing machines), all the principles covered in the book still apply today.<br /><br />Even though the book was written with things in mind that most of us won't necessarily use anymore (such as the problem of threading a projector), the principles are still useful to know when designing modern-day things from cell phones to websites.<br /><br />I would definitely suggest this book to designers, computer scientists, engineers and anyone who might create something for others to use.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12705477810733874356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pzznjfRnFk24wIzsmlEk55hdPzNRCAa0vfUJDSlDKDgmNJ5O4VnLoJgXSyusAXne5GBZBePRjkZP+vA3adlwIA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('S79A1g9A2QpH5HvahLaIZ+MQx4XySHdKd+88mwYOnm3KtzkVGn4E52czvB2y5tVeny2hs0CR/7iF4MWhRpTGdQ==')})
  }

</script>

We\'re rebelling against technology that\'s too complicated, DVD players with too many men<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14713515426958245989\" style=\"display:none\">Ten laws of simplicity for business, technology, and design that teach us how to need less but get more.\n\nFinally, we are learning that simplicity equals sanity. We\'re rebelling against technology that\'s too complicated, DVD players with too many menus, and software accompanied by 75-megabyte &quot;read me&quot; manuals. The iPod\'s clean gadgetry has made simplicity hip. But sometimes we find ourselves caught up in the simplicity paradox: we want something that\'s simple and easy to use, but also does all the complex things we might ever want it to do. In The Laws of Simplicity, John Maeda offers ten laws for balancing simplicity and complexity in business, technology, and design—guidelines for needing less and actually getting more.\n\nMaeda—a professor in MIT\'s Media Lab and a world-renowned graphic designer—explores the question of how we can redefine the notion of &quot;improved&quot; so that it doesn\'t always mean something more, something added on.\n\nMaeda\'s first law of simplicity is &quot;Reduce.&quot; It\'s not necessarily beneficial to add technology features just because we can. And the features that we do have must be organized (Law 2) in a sensible hierarchy so users aren\'t distracted by features and functions they don\'t need. But simplicity is not less just for the sake of less. Skip ahead to Law 9: &quot;Failure: Accept the fact that some things can never be made simple.&quot; Maeda\'s concise guide to simplicity in the digital age shows us how this idea can be a cornerstone of organizations and their products—how it can drive both business and technology. We can learn to simplify without sacrificing comfort and meaning, and we can achieve the balance described in Law 10.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/552.Donald_A_Norman">About Donald A. Norman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/552.Donald_A_Norman"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1227363427p3/552.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/552.Donald_A_Norman">Donald A. Norman
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
906
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer711781210101951240">Donald Arthur Norman is a professor emeritus of cognitive science at the University of California, San Diego and a Professor of Computer Science at Northwestern University, where he also co-directs the dual degree MBA + Engineering degree program between the Kellogg school and Northwestern Engineering. Norman is on numerous company advisory boards, including the editorial board of Encyclopædia Bri</span>
  <span id="freeText711781210101951240" style="display:none">Donald Arthur Norman is a professor emeritus of cognitive science at the University of California, San Diego and a Professor of Computer Science at Northwestern University, where he also co-directs the dual degree MBA + Engineering degree program between the Kellogg school and Northwestern Engineering. Norman is on numerous company advisory boards, including the editorial board of Encyclopædia Britannica. He currently splits his time between consulting, teaching, and writing. He co-founded the Nielsen Norman Group, a consulting group on matters of usability, which also includes Jakob Nielsen and Bruce "Tog" Tognazzini.<br /><br />Many of Norman's books deal mostly with usability or with cognitive psychology. He loves products which are enjoyable to use, a feature which he attributes to putting together emotion and design, or heart and mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="711781210101951240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/18518-the-psychology-of-everyday-things">Quotes from The Design of Eve...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Design is really an act of communication, which means having a deep understanding of the person with whom the designer is communicating.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/584493-design-is-really-an-act-of-communication-which-means-having">57 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Principles of design:<br />1. Use both knowledge in the world and knowledge in the head.<br />2. Simplify the structure of tasks.<br />3. Make things visible: bridge gulfs between Execution and Evaluation.<br />4. Get the mappings right.<br />5. Exploit the power of constraints.<br />6. Design for error.<br />7. When all else fails, standardize.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
